    [Original] SpaceX rocket fireball linked to plume of polluting lithium
    A SpaceX Falcon 9 rocket failure on 19 February 2025 resulted in a fiery re-entry over western Europe, with debris later found in Poland. Scientists from the Leibniz Institute of Atmospheric Physics and the University of Leeds have now established the first direct link between a known piece of space debris and atmospheric pollution, detecting a tenfold increase in lithium levels at approximately 100km altitude. The rocket, composed of aluminium-lithium alloy, vaporized during atmospheric entry, releasing significant metallic particles. Professor Robin Wing noted that while natural meteoritic input contributes 50â€“80g of material daily, the Falcon 9 released about 30kg of lithiumâ€”substantially more. The team used laser detection to identify metal atoms, including aluminium and lithium, in the upper atmosphere. They warn that aluminium and aluminium oxides may interact with the ozone layer, potentially disrupting atmospheric aerosols critical to climate regulation. The long-term environmental impact remains unknown, but researchers caution that such pollution could escalate as SpaceX plans to launch up to one million satellites to support AI data centers. Currently, nearly 30,000 pieces of space debris orbit Earth, posing collision risks to satellites, the International Space Station, and Earth itself. Despite outreach, SpaceX has not responded to requests for comment. Earlier studies suggest 10% of atmospheric aerosols are already contaminated by space debris. Scientists, including Professor Andy Lawrence of the University of Edinburgh, stress that existing space treaties do not address emerging threats like atmospheric pollution, interference with astronomy, or debris falling to Earth. They urge stronger international regulation, noting that space commerce is evolving faster than regulatory frameworks. A 2023 study by NASA, the University of Plymouth, and the University of Texas called for the inclusion of orbital protection in the UNâ€™s sustainable development goals. The findings were published in *Communications Earth & Environment*.

    [Original] Etsy sells second-hand fashion app Depop to eBay for $1.2bn
    Depop, the UK-founded second-hand fashion app popular with Gen Z, is being acquired by eBay for $1.2 billion (Â£890 million), just five years after Etsy purchased it for $1.6 billion. The all-cash deal, expected to close by mid-2025, will see Depop retain its name and branding. eBay aims to leverage the acquisition to reach a younger demographic within the rapidly expanding re-commerce market. Depop, one of the early entrants in the fashion resale space, has seen its value decline amid rising competition, particularly from Vinted, which has gained market share through global expansion and technological improvements.     [Original] US funding for global internet freedom â€˜effectively guttedâ€™
    For nearly two decades, the U.S. State Department and U.S. Agency for Global Media funded the Internet Freedom programme, disbursing over $500 million in the past decadeâ€”including $94 million in 2024â€”to support grassroots technologies enabling users in authoritarian regimes to bypass internet censorship. The programme, managed through the Open Technology Fund (OTF), backed tools like Signal and Tor, as well as advanced circumvention methods such as satellite datacasting and encrypted communication platforms used during Iranâ€™s recent anti-government protests. These technologies allowed activists to share videos of violence and coordinate despite government-imposed internet blackouts. However, the programme has been effectively gutted following the creation of Donald Trumpâ€™s Department of Government Efficiency (Doge) in 2025. Career staff resigned or were dismissed, and no grants were issued in 2025. The OTF won a December lawsuit to restore funding, but the Trump administration is appealing. In January, the U.S. also withdrew from the Freedom Online Coalition, a global digital rights alliance. The cuts jeopardize efforts in countries like Iran, Myanmar, and China, where censorship regimes are intensifying. â€œThe programme was effectively gutted,â€ said a former U.S. official. â€œThey didnâ€™t issue any grants this year.â€ Digital rights experts warn the loss of funding is unsustainable. â€œItâ€™s a massive blow.     [Original] West Virginia Sues Apple, Claiming It Allows Sharing of Child Sexual Abuse Material
    West Virginiaâ€™s attorney general filed a landmark lawsuit against Apple, alleging the company knowingly allowed its iCloud service to store and share child sexual abuse material (CSAM) by refusing to adopt proven detection tools. The 52-page complaint claims Appleâ€™s decision to prioritize user privacy over safety violated state consumer protection laws. â€œApple is using the cloak of privacy in order to mitigate its duties to children and families in this country,â€ said Attorney General John B. McCuskey, demanding the removal of illegal content and cooperation with law enforcement to prosecute offenders. The suit highlights concerns that iCloudâ€™s privacy protections enable the spread of CSAM more easily than on social media platforms like Facebook and Instagram. It seeks monetary damages and changes to Appleâ€™s CSAM detection practices and product design. Apple responded by reaffirming its commitment to user safety and privacy, stating it â€œis innovating every day to combat ever-evolving threatsâ€ and offering parental controls, including a feature that warns children about nudity. However, Apple has faced prior legal challenges. In December 2024, a group of victims sued for over $1.2 billion, a case still pending. A separate 2024 case involving a 9-year-old girl in North Carolina, who received CSAM via iCloud, was largely dismissed. Sarah Gardner, founder of the child safety group Heat Initiative, which helped initiate the December 2024 lawsuit, said, â€œWe need somebody to hold them accountable.â€ Appleâ€™s CSAM reporting record remains starkly low: in 2023, it reported only 267 cases to the National Center for Missing & Exploited Children, compared to Metaâ€™s over 30 million and Googleâ€™s over one million. In 2009, Microsoft created PhotoDNA, a tool adopted by Google and Dropbox, but Apple declined to use it. Internal Apple communications from 2019 revealed executive Eric Friedman admitted the company was â€œthe greatest platform for distributing child pornâ€ due to its privacy-first approach. In 2021, Apple introduced NeuralHash, a detection system, but abandoned it after cybersecurity experts warned it could compromise user privacy and enable government surveillance. The West Virginia lawsuit reflects a broader legal trend challenging tech giants under personal injury liability theories, bypassing Section 230 protections by arguing that platforms are defective products. The case is part of a growing push to hold tech companies accountable for enabling harm despite available safeguards.

    [Original] é˜¿æ ¹å»·ä¸¤å¹´å†…å‡å°‘è¿‘2.2ä¸‡å®¶å…¬å¸
    Data released recently by Argentina's Occupational Risk Supervision Agency shows that the country has reduced nearly 22,000 companies over the past two years. In a report, the agency stated that the number of registered enterprises in Argentina dropped from over 510,000 in November 2023 to over 490,000 in November 2025. During the same period, the number of registered employees fell from 9.857 million to 9.567 million, resulting in the loss of nearly 300,000 jobs. (Xinhua News Agency)

    [Original] Lobbying firm co-founded by Mandelson on brink of collapse
    ShareSaveTheo LeggettBusiness correspondentShareSavePA MediaGlobal Counsel, the lobbying firm co-founded by Peter Mandelson, has confirmed it is preparing to enter administration on Friday. Staff have reportedly been told the move became inevitable when leading clients severed their relationships with the company, after details emerged of apparent links with the convicted sex offender Jeffrey Epstein. Lord Mandelson resigned from the board of Global Counsel in 2024, though he remained a shareholder until earlier this month. His co-founder, Benjamin Wegg-Prosser, stepped down as chief executive on 6 February. Sources close to the company insist its difficulties stem entirely from "the Mandelson legacy" and not from any actions by staff. Global Counsel warned there will be "a significant number of redundancies being made by the administrators when they take control of the company" on Friday. The lobbying firm was set up by Lord Mandelson and Wegg-Prosser after Labour lost the 2010 general election. It became a highly influential lobbying firm in Westminster, advising high-profile clients such as Shell, TikTok and Barclays. The company employs more than 100 people and has offices in Berlin, Brussels, Singapore, Washington DC and Doha. However, its future was thrown into question following the publication of documents from the Epstein files, which cast light on Lord Mandelson's links with the disgraced financier. In a statement on Thursday, Global Counsel said "many clients" had remained loyal to the firm in recent weeks. But it added: "The continuing maelstrom of political and media attention surrounding Peter Mandelson has made it challenging to continue with the business in its current form. "The administrators-in-waiting have already indicated that, in the unlikely event that any ongoing servicing of clients is viable, this will be on a limited basis only." The files also contained mentions of a meeting between Wegg-Prosser and Epstein in 2010, two years after the latter's conviction for soliciting prostitution and soliciting a minor. Epstein died in New York in 2019, while awaiting trial on sex trafficking charges. Lord Mandelson is credited as one of the key architects of the New Labour movement, which came to power with Tony Blair's election victory in 1997. He served in several ministerial posts under Blair and Gordon Brown, as well as being European commissioner for trade from 2004-2008. He was appointed ambassador to the US in December 2024, but was fired in September after the extent of his relationship with Epstein became clear. Earlier this month, two properties linked to Lord Mandelson were searched by police following allegations of misconduct in public office. Lord Mandelson has been contacted for comment but has not responded. The BBC understands his position is that he has not acted in any way criminally and that he was not motivated by financial gain.

    [Original] The Chinese AI app sending Hollywood into a panic
    A new artificial intelligence model, Seedance 2.0, developed by Chinese tech giant ByteDance, has sparked global controversy and excitement in the creative industry. Launched in June 2025 and refined eight months later, the tool generates cinema-quality video with synchronized dialogue, sound effects, and visuals from just a few text prompts. Viral clips featuring characters like Spider-Man, Deadpool, and Will Smith eating spaghettiâ€”complete with a fictional spaghetti monster battleâ€”demonstrated its realism, prompting comparisons to professional film production. Industry experts, including David Kwok of Singaporeâ€™s Tiny Island Productions, praised its ability to produce complex, lifelike action sequences, likening it to having a seasoned cinematographer on board. However, the technology has ignited fierce legal and ethical debates. Major studios Disney and Paramount issued cease-and-desist letters over the use of copyrighted characters, while Japan launched an investigation into ByteDance for alleged copyright violations involving AI-generated anime. Critics argue that AI firms like ByteDance are prioritizing innovation over ethical data use, often training models on copyrighted material without permission. This mirrors past lawsuits, including the New York Timesâ€™ 2023 suit against OpenAI and Microsoft, and Redditâ€™s 2024 case against Perplexity. Despite the backlash, experts like AI ethics researcher Margaret Mitchell emphasize that transparency and licensing mechanisms are more critical than visual fidelity. She stresses the need for clear systems to manage rights and allow content creators to contest misuse. Disneyâ€™s $1 billion deal with OpenAIâ€™s Sora for access to Star Wars, Pixar, and Marvel IP underscores the growing importance of legal frameworks. For small studios, Seedance offers transformative potential. Kwok noted that AI now enables low-budget productionsâ€”such as Asiaâ€™s popular micro-dramas, costing around $140,000 for 80 episodesâ€”to explore ambitious genres like sci-fi and action, previously cost-prohibitive. This democratization of high-quality video creation highlights the modelâ€™s disruptive power. Seedance 2.0 also underscores Chinaâ€™s rising influence in AI. As Beijing invests heavily in chips, robotics, and generative AI, Chinese firms are emerging as global leaders. DeepSeekâ€™s earlier success with a low-cost language model and the timing of AI launches during Chinaâ€™s Lunar New Year holidayâ€”now dubbed an â€œAI holidayâ€â€”signal a broader shift. Analyst Bill Bishop predicts 2026 could mark a turning point for mass AI adoption in China, with AI agents, coding tools, and video creators becoming commonplace.

    [Original] Warmer weather hits profits at British Gas owner
    ShareSaveJosh MartinBusiness reporterShareSaveGetty Images2025 was the UK's warmest year on record, according to Met Office dataWarmer weather in the UK meant more British Gas customers turned down their heating in 2025, contributing to a slump in profits at its owner Centrica. British Gas took a Â£80m hit from the warmer weather, while customers switching to cheaper fixed-rate energy tariffs from variable rates was also blamed for operating profits at its residential supply business falling 39% to Â£163m last year. Despite the shift, chief executive Chris O'Shea said British Gas had grown its customer base for the first time in more than a decade. The number of customers increased to 7.5 million in the UK, after the firm absorbed the customer base of Rebel Energy and Tomato Energy when those suppliers went bust. Taking on those 91,000 customers offset the number of households leaving British Gas to find better deals. The UK had its warmest and sunniest year on record in 2025, according to the Met Office, with an average temperature of 10.09C. "The environment has been challenging, and performance has varied across the business," Mr O'Shea said. Across the whole Centrica group, operating profits fell to Â£814m last year, down from Â£1.55bn in 2024. Hargreaves Lansdown equity analyst Aarin Chiekrie said the number of households with British Gas and its customer satisfaction scores "continue to trend in the right direction". But he added that the wider energy market had affected Centrica's profitability. "Lower commodity prices and lower energy price volatility weighed on performance, causing total profits to fall sharply," Chiekrie said. Dan Coatsworth, head of markets at AJ Bell, said the "momentum" Centrica had enjoyed when gas prices spiked a few years ago "has now been lost". "Centrica, with its healthy exposure to the wholesale market alongside its ownership of the British Gas retail division, benefited from the surge in energy markets in recent years." Centrica said it had paid out more than Â£1bn in dividend payments and share buybacks in 2025. But announcing its full-year results, the company announced it would pause its share buyback programme to focus on investing in the business, including allocating more money to projects including the Sizewell C nuclear power in Suffolk. Shares in Centrica were trading down more than 7% in London on Thursday morning.

    [Original] Why there's no quick fix in sight for the problem of dazzling headlights
    Older and younger drivers across the UK are reporting increasing discomfort and safety concerns due to headlight dazzle, prompting calls for urgent action. Jane Kingsbury, 80, from Cambridge, says she is giving up night driving due to being â€œdazzledâ€ by oncoming vehicles, a sentiment shared by over 90% of her discussion group, who now meet at 1.30pm instead of 7.30pm to avoid dark journeys. The RACâ€™s January 2024 survey of 1,745 drivers found that over half believed the problem has worsened in the past year, with a third saying they feel less safe at night. Emily McGuire, a 30-year-old from Essex, described difficulty distinguishing between main and dipped beams, suffering migraines and temporary blindness after exposure to bright lights, particularly from larger vehicles. Experts like Denise Voon from the College of Optometrists note that complaints about glare have risen sharply in the past 15 years, coinciding with the shift from halogen to LED headlights. LEDs are two to three times brighter, emit a bluer, whiter light, and are more directionalâ€”factors that exacerbate glare. The UK government has acknowledged the issue, with a Transport Research Laboratory (TRL) study finding that glare perception increases significantly at luminance levels above 40,000 candela per sq m, especially when driving uphill or around right-hand bends. Larger vehicles and LED headlamps were perceived as particularly problematic. Despite this, the Department for Transport no longer lists â€œvision affected by dazzling headlightsâ€ as a standalone contributory factor in collision statistics, making trend analysis difficult. While headlight dazzle was a factor in 216 collisions in 2023â€”four fatalâ€”the overall number remains below levels seen in 2014. The government has agreed to commission further research, though it has not yet begun. Critics argue that manufacturers prioritized driver visibility over oncoming traffic safety, though the SMMT and Volvo maintain that headlights meet international standards and that glare is mitigated through regulated beam angles and auto-dimming systems. However, misaligned or illegally retrofitted LED bulbs remain a concern, with the Driver and Vehicle Standards Agency cracking down on non-compliant fittings. The debate continues over whether current regulations and technology are sufficient to address a growing public safety issue.

    [Original] The two farms in Senegal that supply many of the UK's vegetables
    UK shoppers buying spring onions, green beans, corn, and other fresh produce during winter may be consuming goods grown in Senegalâ€™s Saint-Louis region, at the edge of the Sahara. Two British-run farmsâ€”Gâ€™s Fresh and Barfootsâ€”operate on 2,000 hectares of irrigated land, employing 9,000 mostly female workers. The farms, established in the early 2000s by French agronomist Michael Laurent, use water diverted from the Senegal River through a network of canals and pumps to cultivate crops in arid conditions. Gâ€™s Fresh supplies two million bunches of spring onions, 100 tonnes of green beans, and 80 tonnes of radishes weekly to UK supermarkets, with 70% going to the UK. Barfoots, a joint venture with Laurentâ€™s SCL, supplies 55 million corn cobs annually, along with chillis and butternut squash. Harvested produce travels by road to Dakarâ€™s deep-water port, then by container shipâ€”departing weeklyâ€”to Poole, Dorset, a 3,000-mile journey taking six days. The UK imports up to 90% of its fresh produce in winter, with Senegal emerging as a new source due to Mediterranean land competition, droughts in Spain, and post-Brexit shifts. Senegalâ€™s political stability and long-term land leases make it attractive, despite restrictions on foreign land ownership. Barfoots has invested Â£70m, and expansion is driven by consumer demand for year-round availability. While Senegalâ€™s unemployment remains high at 19%, the farms provide significant employment. Workers earn the local minimum wage of 2,500 West African francs ($4.50; Â£3) daily, with bonuses for productivity. Laurent acknowledges wages are low, citing tight profit margins. Gâ€™s Freshâ€™s Derek Wilkinson notes labour costs in Senegal are less than one-third of those in the UK, making imports increasingly competitive even in summer. However, critics like emeritus professor Tim Lang argue that long-distance shipping, though lower in emissions than air freight, still contributes to climate change. He urges consumers to align diets with seasonal availability and support UK-grown produce instead.

    [Original] Microsoft error sees confidential emails exposed to AI tool Copilot
    Microsoft has acknowledged a security flaw in its Microsoft 365 Copilot Chat AI tool that inadvertently accessed and summarized confidential emails from usersâ€™ Drafts and Sent folders, including those marked as confidential. The issue, first reported by Bleeping Computer and confirmed via a Microsoft service alert, occurred despite existing access controls and data protection policies. A Microsoft spokesperson stated the tool â€œcould return content from emails labelled confidential authored by a user and stored within their Draft and Sent Items in Outlook desktop,â€ violating the intended design to exclude protected content. The company confirmed a configuration update has been deployed globally for enterprise customers to resolve the problem. Microsoft emphasized that no unauthorized individuals gained access to the data, and that any processed information remained with the user, including in cases involving sensitive data such as patient information within the NHS. The bug was reportedly identified in January and attributed to a â€œcode issueâ€ affecting the NHS IT support dashboard, which also noted the tool had summarized messages despite sensitivity labels and data loss prevention policies. Experts warn such incidents are inevitable amid the rapid rollout of generative AI features. Gartner analyst Nader Henein said the â€œtorrent of unsubstantiated AI hypeâ€ pressures organizations to adopt new tools before governance frameworks are in place, making it difficult to disable risky features. He noted that organizations often lack the tools to manage new AI capabilities effectively. Cybersecurity expert Professor Alan Woodward of the University of Surrey stressed the need for AI tools to be â€œprivate-by-defaultâ€ and opt-in only, given the inevitability of bugs in fast-moving technologies. While enterprise AI tools like Copilot Chat are designed with stricter security controls, the incident underscores ongoing risks in deploying generative AI in sensitive work environments. Microsoft continues to promote Copilot Chat as a secure solution for workplaces, but the error highlights the challenges of balancing innovation with data protection.

    [Original] Etsy sells second-hand fashion app Depop to eBay for $1.2bn
    Depop, the UK-founded second-hand fashion app popular with Gen Z, is being acquired by eBay for $1.2 billion (Â£890 million), just five years after Etsy purchased it for $1.6 billion. The all-cash deal, expected to close by mid-2025, will see Depop retain its name and branding. eBay aims to leverage the acquisition to reach a younger demographic within the rapidly expanding re-commerce market. Depop, one of the early entrants in the fashion resale space, has seen its value decline amid rising competition, particularly from Vinted, which has gained market share through global expansion and technological improvements. Retail analyst Catherine Shuttleworth noted that Vintedâ€™s superior platform accessibility and user experience have drawn sellers away from Depop, which lacked the â€œfrictionlessâ€ and â€œuniversalâ€ appeal of rivals like eBay and Vinted. Etsy cited Depopâ€™s strong appeal to under-34sâ€”nearly 90% of its seven million active buyersâ€”and over three million active sellers as key assets. Despite this, Etsy has scaled back on its acquisition strategy, selling off assets like Elo7 in 2023 and Reverb in 2024 to refocus on its core crafts and handmade goods business. The company has faced declining demand and intense competition from fast-fashion platforms like Shein and Temu, which offer lower prices and faster delivery. Etsyâ€™s 2025 financial results showed revenues rose 8% year-on-year to $11.1 billion. The Depop sale has boosted investor confidence, with Etsyâ€™s share price rising over 15% in after-hours trading and eBayâ€™s up nearly 6.5%. eBayâ€™s CEO, Jamie Ianonne, highlighted that used clothing is one of the companyâ€™s â€œfastest-growingâ€ categories, and the acquisition is expected to accelerate growth in that segment. Etsy CEO Kruti Patel Goyal expressed confidence that Depop is â€œwell-positioned for its next phase of growthâ€ under eBayâ€™s ownership.

    [Original] Reeves fixated on 'dysfunctional' borrowing rules, says IFS
    ShareSaveJennifer McKiernanPolitical reporterShareSavePA MediaChancellor Rachel ReevesChancellor Rachel Reeves is "fixated" on borrowing rules which are contributing to "dysfunctional policymaking," according to a leading think tank. The Institute for Fiscal Studies (IFS) said the chancellor's approach "needs a rethink" to move away from constraints that "require some measure of borrowing or debt to be below a particular threshold in a specified year". Reeves set her so-called fiscal rules when Labour came to power, which determine how much "headroom" the government has for its tax and spending plans. The Treasury said: "The government's non-negotiable fiscal rules help to keep interest rates low while also prioritising investment to support long-term growth." But Ben Zaranko, associate director at the IFS, said the current framework was judging economic sustainability by a system that is "boiled down to to a single number". At the Autumn Budget, the Office for Budget Responsibility forecast that the amount of headroom the government has against its borrowing rules will be Â£22bn in five years' time. This was more than earlier forecasts of Â£9.9bn. But research from the IFS, funded by the Joseph Rowntree Foundation, suggests that instead of "passâ€“fail fiscal rules", the UK "would be better served by a new framework based around a set of 'fiscal traffic lights'". These would be used to monitor performance against high-level objectives and principles set out at the start of each parliament. Zaranko said: "Moving to a broader set of fiscal indicators, assessed according to a traffic light system, would provide a better picture of the government's overall fiscal position, and reduce the incentive for governments to contort policy in pursuit of a particular 'headroom' number." The IFS report said that the current framework is not delivering sustainable public finances and aggressive "gaming" of rolling targets and frequent changes to the rules have undermined their credibility. IFS director Helen Miller urged a debate on public finances sooner rather than later, ahead of the next general election, because the current fiscal framework "isn't delivering". "A broad traffic light assessment of the public finances would make governments' multiple objectives â€“ and the associated trade-offs faced â€“ much more transparent and explicit," she said. The Treasury said: "In the Budget, we doubled the fiscal headroom, and we are cutting borrowing more than any other G7 country with borrowing forecast to be the lowest in six years as share of GDP."     [Original] 'We're still on edge': Toy firms look to US Supreme Court as tariffs hit profits
    Rick Woldenberg, CEO of educational toy company Learning Resources, drew attention at New Yorkâ€™s annual Toy Fair as a symbol of corporate resistance to U.S. trade policy, having sued the Trump administration over sweeping global tariffs. The litigation, sparked by tariffs that at one point reached 145% on Chinese imports, targeted the economic upheaval that hit industries reliant on Chinese manufacturing, including toys. While the White House has since exempted some products and reduced average tariffs on Chinese goods to around 20%, uncertainty persists. At the fair, Woldenberg was greeted by peers who praised his legal challenge, with the Supreme Court expected to rule on the case as early as Friday. The tariffs significantly impacted profits, prompting many firms to raise prices. Basic Fun, which saw a 65% profit decline last year, increased its Tonka truck price from $30 to $35â€”a hike it expects to maintain through 2026. Glo Pals, a sensory toy company, raised its best-selling water-activated light-up cubes from $10.99 to $12.99 in April, its first price increase in six years. Co-founder Anna Barker said the company hopes to avoid further hikes but warned that shifting tariff policies could disrupt plans. â€œItâ€™s an economic landscape that is completely temperamental,â€ she said, noting that uncertainty remains despite current stability. Harvard Business School professor Alberto Cavallo found that while cheaper goods saw faster price increases, tariffs overall had â€œlittle impactâ€ on toy prices. Still, companies remain cautious. Jay Foreman of Basic Fun said, â€œYou cannot go to sleep on this president,â€ and expects to remain on edge for at least three more years. If the Supreme Court rules against the tariffs, the government may need to refund billions collected, potentially allowing companies to lower prices. Woldenberg said his firm is betting on â€œfurther reliefâ€ and plans to pass savings on to consumers quickly. Tim Hislop, co-founder of UK-based Floss & Rock, said he doesnâ€™t expect refunds for past paymentsâ€”accounting for over half his U.S. revenueâ€”but a negative ruling could still reduce future costs. â€œI have a little prayer every night,â€ he joked, underscoring the ongoing anxiety. The White House has stated it would use alternative tools to impose tariffs if the current measures are struck down.

    [Original] Trump Administration Uses â€˜Equal Timeâ€™ Rule to Reshape Late-Night TV
    In October 2006, then-California Republican Governor Arnold Schwarzenegger appeared on â€œThe Tonight Show With Jay Leno,â€ prompting a Democratic opponent to file a complaint with the Federal Communications Commission (F.C.C.) over the networkâ€™s failure to offer equal airtime to rival candidates under the decades-old equal time rule. At the time, the F.C.C. under President George W. Bush exempted news programs from the rule, a precedent that extended to late-night talk shows for two decades. However, under the Trump administration, F.C.C. Chairman Brendan Carr has reversed course, asserting that the equal time rule now applies to late-night hostsâ€”despite the showsâ€™ long-standing political leanings and the fact that most broadcast networks book Democrats over Republicans. This shift has sparked controversy, particularly after Stephen Colbert, host of CBSâ€™s â€œThe Late Show,â€ criticized both his network and the F.C.C. for blocking his planned interview with Democratic Texas Senate primary candidate James Talarico. His actions have included investigating â€œThe Viewâ€ and prompting ABC to pull â€œJimmy Kimmel Live!â€ from the air for nearly a week in September. Carr maintains the goal is to increase speech, not limit it, citing Congressâ€™s original intent to prevent media bias in elections. The F.C.C. holds authority over broadcast licenses, which are essential for national distribution, and Carr has warned that violations could jeopardize license renewals. With major broadcasters like CBSâ€™s parent company, Paramount, seeking government approval for a merger with Warner Bros. Discovery, the stakes are high. As late-night television faces declining ratings, shrinking ad revenue, and competition from streaming and podcasts, the F.C.C.â€™s renewed enforcement of outdated rules raises questions about their relevance in todayâ€™s media landscape. Colbertâ€™s show is set to end in May, potentially leaving NBCâ€™s â€œThe Tonight Showâ€ as the sole remaining broadcast network late-night program.

    [Original] More than 90 deaths this season: Are we seeing more avalanches?
    Avalanche risks have surged across the Alps and in California, resulting in multiple fatalities and widespread disruptions. In California, eight skiers died and one remains missing following an avalanche during the peak ski season, despite no exceptional snowfallâ€”highlighting dangers from snow droughts followed by intense precipitation, which can prevent new snow from bonding to older layers. In Europe, two major storms in a week brought heavy snow and strong winds, triggering high avalanche warnings across an unusually large portion of the Alps. Three Britons were among dozens killed this season, with 95 fatalities reported so farâ€”exceeding the 70 in 2023â€“24 and 87 in 2022â€“23, though still below the 131 deaths in 2020â€“21 and 147 in 2017â€“18. Recent snowfalls caused evacuations in Switzerland and northern Italy, power outages, and a train derailment in Valais. Experts point to climate change as a contributing factor, with Simon Mason of SEI US noting that climate change may increase the frequency of sharp precipitation events followed by dry periods. However, climatologist Christoph Marthy noted the current Alpine winterâ€”marked by prolonged dry spells and high pressureâ€”contrasts with typical climate change projections. Benjamin Zweifel of the Swiss Federal Institute for Snow and Avalanche Research emphasized that weak snowpacks, caused by long periods without snow and low temperatures, were the primary issue, not just recent storms. Wet snow avalanches, driven by rain, sunshine, or warming temperatures, are becoming more frequent and harder to predict. Research suggests warmer, wetter snow may reduce survival chances and increase blunt trauma risks due to thinner snow cover. Four key destabilizing factors are emerging: drought-to-heavy-snow transitions, precipitation intensity, wetter snow, and melting. Increased off-piste skiingâ€”now practiced by 25% of skiersâ€”has raised risk, as these areas lack the safety measures of marked pistes, which are often protected by avalanche blasting and monitoring. While forecasting and safety protocols have improved, no area is risk-free. Resorts like Tignes and Val dâ€™IsÃ¨re imposed lockdowns, while La Plagne promotes safety gear, professional guides, and drone-assisted rescue. Guides like Blaise Agresti and Thomas Hager stress the importance of local knowledge, avalanche forecasts, and carrying airbags, which are not mandatory but can improve survival. Despite higher fatalities in France and Italy (25 each) compared to Switzerland (13), experts believe modern systems have mitigated worse outcomes. The worst may be over, as improved weather is expected from Friday, allowing for safety work and snowpack stabilizationâ€”though sunny, south-facing slopes may see increased off-piste risks.

    [Original] Veteran dissident who refused exile released
    ShareSaveJaroslav LukivShareSaveReutersIn September 2025, Mikola Statkevich was seen sitting in no man's land at Lithuania's border crossing, after refusing to leave BelarusBelarus has released jailed veteran opposition politician Mikola Statkevich, who refused to go into exile last year in a US-brokered prisoner release. The 69-year-old was now at home and recovering from a stroke which had affected his speech, his wife Marina Adamovich said. Statkevich spent more than five years behind bars after receiving a 14-year sentence for organising what the authorities called "mass riots" in 2020-21. He had run unsuccessfully against Alexander Lukashenko in a 2010 presidential poll. More than 1,000 political prisoners remain in jail in Belarus, rights groups say. "He is at home, recovering," Adamovich said on Thursday. "I really hope that everything will be fine. The main problem at the moment is his speech. It's bad, it's difficult to have a conversation." Meanwhile, exiled Belarusian leader Svetlana Tikhanovskaya said she was relieved that Statkevich "is free and able to hug his wife, who waited for him for so long". "I want to thank everyone who keeps fighting to release all political prisoners in Belarus. We must continue until every last one is free," she added in a post on X. Statkevich was one of 52 prisoners freed last year in Belarus, following an appeal from US President Donald Trump. They were jailed in 2020-21, when Lukashenko brutally stamped out protests after claiming victory in presidential elections that were widely condemned as rigged. Most agreed to be sent abroad into exile. Statkevich was the only one who refused to leave the country. In exchange for the prisoner release, the US pledged to relieve some sanctions on Belarusian airline Belavia, allowing it to buy parts for its aircraft. Belarus is a close ally of Russia, which launched a full-scale invasion of Ukraine in February 2022. Lukashenko has been in power in Belarus since 1994, quashing all opposition.

    [Original] Hamas is reasserting control in Gaza despite its heavy losses fighting Israel
    Following a U.S.-imposed ceasefire that halted the Gaza War in October, Hamas has reasserted control over more than 90% of areas it previously governed, according to activist Mohammed Diab. The group has reinstated its police, judiciary, and tax collection systems, requiring citizens to obtain identity cards, health services, and court access through Hamas authorities.     [Original] Macron and Meloni clash over killing of French far-right activist in Lyon

The reforms, which Milei has championed as essential to economic revitalization, include extending the standard workday from eight to 12 hours, easing employer access to dismiss workers, reducing severance pay, and restricting the right to strike by expanding the definition of essential services. Milei, a libertarian, has long criticized Argentinaâ€™s labour regulations as economic inhibitors, famously using a chainsaw at rallies to symbolize his reform agenda. The administration argues the changes will attract foreign investment and boost profitability by reducing regulatory burdens. Argentinaâ€™s economy, already strained by inflation exceeding 200% at the time of Mileiâ€™s election, remains a central concern.
